<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Shaktism</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3_Shaktism_goddesses_Devi_collage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1337" data-file-width="2908" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/3_Shaktism_goddesses_Devi_collage.jpg/360px-3_Shaktism_goddesses_Devi_collage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/3_Shaktism_goddesses_Devi_collage.jpg/540px-3_Shaktism_goddesses_Devi_collage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/3_Shaktism_goddesses_Devi_collage.jpg/720px-3_Shaktism_goddesses_Devi_collage.jpg 2x" width="360"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3_Shaktism_goddesses_Devi_collage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shaktism is a Goddess-centric tradition of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klostermaier2010p30_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klostermaier2010p30-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood174_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-flood174-2">[2]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Shaktism</b> (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>: <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit Transliteration">Śāktaṃ</span></i>, <span lang="sa" xml:lang="sa">शाक्तं</span>; lit., "doctrine of energy, power, the Goddess") is a major tradition of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, wherein the metaphysical reality is considered feminine and the <a href="/wiki/Devi" title="Devi">Devi</a> (goddess) is supreme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klostermaier2010p30_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Klostermaier2010p30-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood174_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-flood174-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> It includes a variety of goddesses, all considered aspects of the same supreme goddess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klostermaier2010p30_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Klostermaier2010p30-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melton2010p2600_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melton2010p2600-4">[4]</a></sup> Shaktism has different sub-traditions, ranging from those focussed on gracious <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a> to fierce <a href="/wiki/Kali" title="Kali">Kali</a>, and some Shakti sub-traditions associate their goddess with <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannicashakti_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannicashakti-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sruti" title="Sruti">Sruti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smriti" title="Smriti">Smriti</a> texts of Hinduism are an important historical framework of the Shaktism tradition. In addition, it reveres the texts <i><a href="/wiki/Devi_Mahatmya" title="Devi Mahatmya">Devi Mahatmya</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Devi-Bhagavata_Purana" title="Devi-Bhagavata Purana">Devi-Bhagavata Purana</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Shakta_Upanishads" title="Shakta Upanishads">Shakta Upanishads</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Devi_Upanishad" title="Devi Upanishad">Devi Upanishad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The <i>Devi Mahatmya</i> in particular, is considered in Shaktism to be as important as the <i><a href="/wiki/Bhagavad_Gita" title="Bhagavad Gita">Bhagavad Gita</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shaktism is known for its various sub-traditions of <a href="/wiki/Tantra" title="Tantra">Tantra</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> as well as a galaxy of goddesses with respective systems. It consists of the Vidyapitha and <a href="/wiki/Kulam%C4%81rga" title="Kulamārga">Kulamārga</a>. The pantheon of goddesses in Shaktism grew after the <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_Buddhism_in_India" title="Decline of Buddhism in India">decline of Buddhism in India</a>, wherein <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> goddesses were combined to form the <a href="/wiki/Mahavidya" title="Mahavidya">Mahavidya</a>, a list of ten goddesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The most common aspects of Devi found in Shaktism include <a href="/wiki/Durga" title="Durga">Durga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kali" title="Kali">Kali</a>, Amba, <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parvati" title="Parvati">Parvati</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tripurasundari" title="Tripurasundari">Tripurasundari</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melton2010p2600_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Melton2010p2600-4">[4]</a></sup> The goddess-focussed tradition is particularly popular in <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tripura" title="Tripura">Tripura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> and the neighboring regions, which it celebrates through festivals such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Durga_puja" title="Durga puja">Durga puja</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannicashakti_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannicashakti-5">[5]</a></sup> Shaktism's ideas have influenced <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shaivism" title="Shaivism">Shaivism</a> traditions, with the goddess considered the <i>Shakti</i> of Vishnu and Shiva respectively, and revered prominently in numerous <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple" title="Hindu temple">Hindu temples</a> and festivals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flood174_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-flood174-2">[2]</a></sup> Within Hinduism an estimated 30 million or 3.2% followers are Shaktas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_history">Origins and history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origins and history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>One of the earliest evidence of reverence for the feminine aspect of God in Hinduism appears in chapter 10.125 of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rig_Veda" title="Rig Veda">Rig Veda</a></i>, also called the Devi Suktam hymn:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJune_McDaniel200490_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJune_McDaniel200490-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown199826_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown199826-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hymn_125_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hymn_125-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote style="background-color:none;margin-right:5em;margin-left:0em;border-left:solid 6px #FFE0BB;padding:1.0em">
<div class="poem">
<p>I am the Queen, the gatherer-up of treasures, most thoughtful, first of those who merit worship.<br/>
     Thus gods have established me in many places with many homes to enter and abide in.<br/>
Through me alone all eat the food that feeds them, – each man who sees, breathes, hears the word outspoken.<br/>
     They know it not, yet I reside in the essence of the Universe. Hear, one and all, the truth as I declare it.<br/>
I, verily, myself announce and utter the word that gods and men alike shall welcome.<br/>
     I make the man I love exceeding mighty, make him nourished, a sage, and one who knows Brahman.<br/>
I bend the bow for Rudra [Shiva], that his arrow may strike, and slay the hater of devotion.<br/>
     I rouse and order battle for the people, I created Earth and Heaven and reside as their Inner Controller.<br/>
On the world's summit I bring forth sky the Father: my home is in the waters, in the ocean as Mother.<br/>
     Thence I pervade all existing creatures, as their Inner Supreme Self, and manifest them with my body.<br/>
I created all worlds at my will, without any higher being, and permeate and dwell within them.<br/>
     The eternal and infinite consciousness is I, it is my greatness dwelling in everything.</p>
</div>
<p>–<a href="/wiki/Devi" title="Devi">Devi</a> Sukta, <i>Rigveda 10.125.3 – 10.125.8</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJune_McDaniel200490_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJune_McDaniel200490-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown199826_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown199826-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hymn_125_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hymn_125-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The Vedic literature reveres various goddesses, but far less frequently than gods <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agni" title="Agni">Agni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soma_(deity)" title="Soma (deity)">Soma</a>. Yet, they are declared equivalent aspects of gender neutral <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Prajapati" title="Prajapati">Prajapati</a> and <a href="/wiki/Purusha" title="Purusha">Purusha</a>. The goddesses oft mentioned in the Vedic layers of text include the Ushas (dawn), Vac (speech, wisdom), Sarasvati (as river), Prithivi (earth), Nirriti (annihilator), Shraddha (faith, confidence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melton2010p2600_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Melton2010p2600-4">[4]</a></sup> Goddesses such as Uma appear in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upanishad" title="Upanishad">Upanishads</a> as another aspect of Brahman and the knower of ultimate knowledge, such as in section 3 and 4 of the ancient <i><a href="/wiki/Kena_Upanishad" title="Kena Upanishad">Kena Upanishad</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pauldeussen34_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-pauldeussen34-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charleskenaprose_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-charleskenaprose-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hymns to goddesses are in the ancient Hindu epic <i>Mahabharata</i>, particularly in the later (100 to 300 CE) added <i>Harivamsa</i> section of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FulkersonBriggs139_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FulkersonBriggs139-17">[17]</a></sup> The archaeological and textual evidence implies, states Thomas Coburn, that the Goddess had become as much a part of the Hindu tradition, as God, by about the third or fourth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoburn20027_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoburn20027-18">[18]</a></sup> The literature on Shakti theology grew in ancient India, climaxing in one of the most important texts of Shaktism called the <i>Devi Mahatmya</i>. This text, states C. Mackenzie Brown – a professor of Religion, is both a culmination of centuries of Indian ideas about the divine feminine, as well as a foundation for the literature and spirituality focussed on the feminine transcendence in centuries that followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FulkersonBriggs139_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FulkersonBriggs139-17">[17]</a></sup> The <i>Devi-Mahatmya</i> is not the earliest literary fragment attesting to the existence of devotion to a goddess figure, states <a href="/wiki/Thomas_B._Coburn" title="Thomas B. Coburn">Thomas B. Coburn</a> – a professor of Religious Studies, but "it is surely the earliest in which the object of worship is conceptualized as Goddess, with a capital G".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoburn199116_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoburn199116-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other important texts of Shaktism include the <i><a href="/wiki/Shakta_Upanishads" title="Shakta Upanishads">Shakta Upanishads</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrishna_Warrier1999ix-x_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrishna_Warrier1999ix-x-20">[20]</a></sup> as well as Shakta-oriented <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Upa Puranic literature</a> such as the <i>Devi Purana</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Kalika_Purana" title="Kalika Purana">Kalika Purana</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhattacharyya1996164_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharyya1996164-21">[21]</a></sup> the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lalita_sahasranama" title="Lalita sahasranama">Lalita Sahasranama</a></i> (from the <i><a href="/wiki/Brahmanda_Purana" title="Brahmanda Purana">Brahmanda Purana</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDikshitar19991.E2.80.9336_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDikshitar19991.E2.80.9336-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown19988.2C_17.2C_10.2C_21.2C_320_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown19988.2C_17.2C_10.2C_21.2C_320-23">[23]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Tripura_Upanishad" title="Tripura Upanishad">Tripura Upanishad</a></i> is historically the most complete introduction to Shakta Tantrism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xiii.E2.80.93xiv_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xiii.E2.80.93xiv-24">[24]</a></sup> distilling into its 16 verses almost every important topic in Shakta Tantra tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xvi_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xvi-25">[25]</a></sup> Along with the <i>Tripura Upanishad</i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Tripuratapini_Upanishad" title="Tripuratapini Upanishad">Tripuratapini Upanishad</a></i> has attracted scholarly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhasya" title="Bhasya">bhasya</a> (commentary) in the second half of 2nd-millennium, such as by <a href="/wiki/Bhaskararaya" title="Bhaskararaya">Bhaskararaya</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks199037-38_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks199037-38-26">[26]</a></sup> and by Ramanand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990221_with_note_64_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990221_with_note_64-27">[27]</a></sup> These texts link the Shakti Tantra tradition as a Vedic attribute,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDasgupta19963_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDasgupta19963-28">[28]</a></sup> however this link has been contested by scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xiii.E2.80.93xiv.2C_xvi.2C_21_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xiii.E2.80.93xiv.2C_xvi.2C_21-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hughurban_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-hughurban-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:303px;max-width:303px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:137px;max-width:137px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ardhanari.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="764" data-file-width="447" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Ardhanari.jpg/135px-Ardhanari.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Ardhanari.jpg/203px-Ardhanari.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Ardhanari.jpg/270px-Ardhanari.jpg 2x" width="135"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:God_marriage_AS.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="417" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/God_marriage_AS.jpg/160px-God_marriage_AS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/God_marriage_AS.jpg/240px-God_marriage_AS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/God_marriage_AS.jpg/320px-God_marriage_AS.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">In Shakta theology, the feminine and masculine are interdependent realities, represented with <a href="/wiki/Ardhanarishvara" title="Ardhanarishvara">Ardhanarishvara</a> icon. Left: A 5th century art work representing this idea at the <a href="/wiki/Elephanta_Caves" title="Elephanta Caves">Elephanta Caves</a>; Right: a painting of Ardhanarishvara.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shaktas conceive the Goddess as the supreme, ultimate, eternal reality of all existence, or same as the Brahman concept of Hinduism. She is considered to be simultaneously the source of all creation, its embodiment and the energy that animates and governs it, and that into which everything will ultimately dissolve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhattacharyya19961_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharyya19961-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melton2010p2600_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Melton2010p2600-4">[4]</a></sup> According to V. R. Ramachandra Dikshitar – a professor of Indian history, in Shaktism theology "Brahman is static Shakti and Shakti is dynamic Brahman."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDikshitar199977-78_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDikshitar199977-78-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shaktism views the Devi as the source, essence and substance of everything in creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melton2010p2600_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Melton2010p2600-4">[4]</a></sup> Its texts such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Devi-Bhagavata_Purana" title="Devi-Bhagavata Purana">Devi-Bhagavata Purana</a></i> states:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I am Manifest Divinity, Unmanifest Divinity, and Transcendent Divinity. I am <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Saraswati" title="Saraswati">Saraswati</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parvati" title="Parvati">Parvati</a>. I am the Sun and I am the Stars, and I am also the Moon. I am all animals and birds, and I am the outcaste as well, and the thief. I am the low person of dreadful deeds, and the great person of excellent deeds. I am Female, I am Male in the form of Shiva.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[a]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Shaktism's focus on the Divine Feminine does not imply a rejection of masculine. It rejects male-female, soul-body, transcendent-immanent dualism, considering nature as divine. Devi is considered to be the cosmos itself – she is the embodiment of energy, matter and soul, the motivating force behind all action and existence in the material universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> Yet in Shaktism, states C. MacKenzie Brown, the masculine and the feminine are aspects of the divine, transcendent reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown1991217_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown1991217-36">[35]</a></sup> In Hindu iconography, the cosmic dynamic of masculine-feminine interdependence and equivalence, is expressed in the half-Shakti, half-Shiva deity known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ardhanari" title="Ardhanari">Ardhanari</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYadav2001_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYadav2001-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>The philosophical premises in many Shakta texts, states June McDaniel – a professor of Religious Studies, is syncretism of <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a> schools of <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a>, called <i>Shaktadavaitavada</i> (literally, the path of nondualistic Shakti).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJune_McDaniel200489.E2.80.9391_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJune_McDaniel200489.E2.80.9391-38">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Devi_Gita">Devi Gita</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Devi Gita">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The seventh book of the <i>Devi-Bhagavata Purana</i> presents the theology of Shaktism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERocher1986170_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERocher1986170-39">[38]</a></sup> This book is called <i>Devi Gita</i>, or the "Song of the Goddess".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERocher1986170_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERocher1986170-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown19981-2.2C_85-98_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown19981-2.2C_85-98-40">[39]</a></sup> The Goddess explains she is the Brahman that created the world, asserting the Advaita premise that spiritual liberation occurs when one fully comprehends the identity of one's soul and the Brahman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERocher1986170_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERocher1986170-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown199812-17_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown199812-17-41">[40]</a></sup> This knowledge, asserts the Goddess, comes from detaching self from the world and meditating on one's own soul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERocher1986170_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERocher1986170-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman2015131-138_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman2015131-138-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Devi Gita</i>, like the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>, is a condensed philosophical treatise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEC_Mackenzie_Brown1990179-198_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEC_Mackenzie_Brown1990179-198-43">[42]</a></sup> It presents the divine female as a powerful and compassionate creator, pervader and protector of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown19981-3_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown19981-3-44">[43]</a></sup> She is presented in the opening chapter of the <i>Devi Gita</i> as the benign and beautiful world-mother, called <i>Bhuvaneshvari</i> (literally, ruler of the universe).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman201426-28_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman201426-28-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEC_Mackenzie_Brown1990179-198_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEC_Mackenzie_Brown1990179-198-43">[42]</a></sup> Thereafter, the text presents its theological and philosophical teachings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown19981-3_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown19981-3-44">[43]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #FFE0BB;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p><b>The soul and the Goddess</b></p>
<div class="poem">
<p>[My sacred syllable ह्रीम्] transcends,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[b]</a></sup><br/>
the distinction of name and named,<br/>
beyond all dualities.<br/>
It is whole,<br/>
infinite <a href="/wiki/Satcitananda" title="Satcitananda">being, consciousness and bliss</a>.<br/>
One should meditate on that reality,<br/>
within the flaming light of consciousness.<br/>
Fixing the mind upon me,<br/>
as the Goddess transcending all space and time,<br/>
One quickly merges with me by realizing,<br/>
the oneness of the soul and <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>.</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<i>Devi Gita</i>, Transl: Lynn Foulston, Stuart Abbott<br/>
<i>Devibhagavata Purana</i>, Book 7<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynn_FoulstonStuart_Abbott200974-75_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynn_FoulstonStuart_Abbott200974-75-49">[47]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>The <i>Devi Gita</i> describes the Devi (or Goddess) as "universal, cosmic energy" resident within each individual. It thus weaves in the terminology of <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown19981-3_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown19981-3-44">[43]</a></sup> The text is suffused with <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a> ideas, wherein nonduality is emphasized, all dualities are declared as incorrect, and interconnected oneness of all living being's soul with Brahman is held as the liberating knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown19981-3.2C_12-17_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECheever_Mackenzie_Brown19981-3.2C_12-17-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman20159.2C_34.2C_89-90.2C_131-138_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman20159.2C_34.2C_89-90.2C_131-138-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynn_FoulstonStuart_Abbott200915-16_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynn_FoulstonStuart_Abbott200915-16-52">[50]</a></sup> However, adds Tracy Pintchman – a professor of Religious Studies and Hinduism, <i>Devi Gita</i> incorporates Tantric ideas giving the Devi a form and motherly character rather than the gender-neutral concept of Adi Shankara's Advaita Vedanta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman20149-10_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman20149-10-53">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tantra">Tantra</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Tantra">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sub-traditions of Shaktism include "Tantra", which refers to techniques, practices and ritual grammar involving <i><a href="/wiki/Mantra" title="Mantra">mantra</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Yantra" title="Yantra">yantra</a></i>, <i>nyasa</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mudra" title="Mudra">mudra</a></i> and certain elements of traditional <a href="/wiki/Kundalini_yoga" title="Kundalini yoga">kundalini yoga</a>, typically practiced under the guidance of a qualified <a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a> after due initiation (<i><a href="/wiki/Diksha" title="Diksha">diksha</a></i>) and oral instruction to supplement various written sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks199047-72_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks199047-72-54">[52]</a></sup> There has been a historic debate between Shakta theologians on whether its tantric practices are Vedic or non-Vedic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xii_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xii-55">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xiii.E2.80.93xiv.2C_xvi.2C_21_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xiii.E2.80.93xiv.2C_xvi.2C_21-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hughurban_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-hughurban-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The roots of Shakta Tantrism are unclear, probably ancient and independent of the Vedic tradition of Hinduism. The interaction between Vedic and Tantric traditions trace back to at least the sixth century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xii_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xii-55">[53]</a></sup> and the surge in Tantra tradition developments during the late medieval period, states Geoffrey Samuel, were a means to confront and cope with Islamic invasions and political instability in and after 14th-century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Principal_deities">Principal deities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Principal deities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Durga_Loro_Jonggrang_copy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1086" data-file-width="720" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Durga_Loro_Jonggrang_copy.jpg/180px-Durga_Loro_Jonggrang_copy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Durga_Loro_Jonggrang_copy.jpg/270px-Durga_Loro_Jonggrang_copy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Durga_Loro_Jonggrang_copy.jpg/360px-Durga_Loro_Jonggrang_copy.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Durga_Loro_Jonggrang_copy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 9th-century Durga Shakti idol, victorious over demon Mahishasura, in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shaktas approach the Devi in many forms; however, they are all considered to be but diverse aspects of the one supreme goddess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsley1987_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsley1987-58">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKali2003149_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKali2003149-59">[57]</a></sup> The primary Devi form worshiped by a Shakta devotee is his or her <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ishta-deva" title="Ishta-deva">ishta-devi</a></i>, that is a personally selected Devi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> The selection of this deity can depend on many factors, such as family tradition, regional practice, guru lineage and personal resonance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsley1987102-104_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsley1987102-104-61">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some forms of the goddess are widely known in the Hindu world. The common goddesses of Shaktism, popular in the Hindu thought at least by about mid 1st-millennium CE, include Durga, Kali, Amba, Tripurasundari, Lakshmi (and her avatars such as Radha, Sita), Saraswati and Parvati (Uma).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsley19871-5_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsley19871-5-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melton2010p2600_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Melton2010p2600-4">[4]</a></sup> The rarer forms of Devi found among tantric Shakta are the Mahavidyas, particularly Tara, Bhairavi, Chhinnamasta, Kamala and Buvaneshwari.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsley1987161-165_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsley1987161-165-63">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsley1998_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsley1998-64">[62]</a></sup> Other major goddess groups include the <i><a href="/wiki/Matrikas" title="Matrikas">Sapta-Matrika</a></i> ("Seven Little Mothers"), "who are the energies of different major gods, and described as assisting the great Shakta Devi in her fight with demons", and the 64 <i><a href="/wiki/Yogini" title="Yogini">Yoginis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhattacharyya1996126_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharyya1996126-65">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tantric_traditions">Tantric traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Tantric traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vidy.C4.81p.C4.AB.E1.B9.ADha">Vidyāpīṭha</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vidyāpīṭha">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Vidyāpīṭha is subdivided into Vāmatantras, Yāmalatantras, and Śaktitantras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kulam.C4.81rga">Kulamārga</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Kulamārga">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kulam%C4%81rga" title="Kulamārga">Kulamārga</a> preserves some of the distinctive features of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C4%81p%C4%81lika" title="Kāpālika">Kāpālika</a> tradition, from which it is derived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup> It is subdivided into four subcategories of texts based on the goddesses Kuleśvarī, Kubjikā, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C4%81l%C4%AB" title="Kālī">Kālī</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tripura_Sundari" title="Tripura Sundari">Tripurasundarī</a> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Trika" title="Trika">Trika</a> texts are closely related to the Kuleśvarī texts and can be considered as part of the Kulamārga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship">Worship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Worship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Shaktism encompasses a nearly endless variety of beliefs and practices – from primitive animism to philosophical speculation of the highest order – that seek to access the Shakti (Divine Energy or Power) that is believed to be the Devi's nature and form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESubramuniyaswami20021211_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESubramuniyaswami20021211-70">[68]</a></sup> Its two largest and most visible schools are the <i>Srikula</i> (family of <i><a href="/wiki/Sri" title="Sri">Sri</a></i>), strongest in <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">South India</a>, and the <i>Kalikula</i> (family of <i><a href="/wiki/Kali" title="Kali">Kali</a></i>), which prevails in northern and eastern India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESubramuniyaswami20021211_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESubramuniyaswami20021211-70">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Srikula:_family_of_Sri">Srikula: family of Sri</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Srikula: family of Sri">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lalita_sm.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="882" data-file-width="672" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Lalita_sm.JPG/170px-Lalita_sm.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Lalita_sm.JPG/255px-Lalita_sm.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Lalita_sm.JPG/340px-Lalita_sm.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lalita_sm.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sri Lalita-Tripurasundari enthroned with her left foot upon the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sri_Chakra" title="Sri Chakra">Sri Chakra</a>, holding her traditional symbols, the sugarcane bow, flower arrows, noose and goad.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <i>Srikula</i> (family of <i>Sri</i>) tradition (<i><a href="/wiki/Sampradaya" title="Sampradaya">sampradaya</a></i>) focuses worship on Devi in the form of the goddess <i><a href="/wiki/Tripura_Sundari" title="Tripura Sundari">Lalita-Tripura Sundari</a></i>, who is regarded as the Great Goddess (<i>Mahadevi</i>). Rooted in first-millennium Uttarakhand, Himachal Pradesh and Kashmir Valley, Srikula became a force in South India no later than the seventh century, and is today the prevalent form of Shaktism practiced in South Indian regions such as <a href="/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh" title="Andhra Pradesh">Andhra Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a> and Tamil areas of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks1992back_cover_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks1992back_cover-71">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Srikula's best-known school is <a href="/wiki/Shri_Vidya" title="Shri Vidya">Srividya</a>, "one of Shakta Tantrism's most influential and theologically sophisticated movements." Its central symbol, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sri_Chakra" title="Sri Chakra">Sri Chakra</a></i>, is probably the most famous visual image in all of Hindu Tantric tradition. Its literature and practice is perhaps more systematic than that of any other Shakta sect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xiii_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks1990xiii-72">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Srividya largely views the Goddess as "benign [<i>saumya</i>] and beautiful [<i>saundarya</i>]" (in contrast to Kalikula's focus on "terrifying [<i>ugra</i>] and horrifying [<i>ghora</i>]" goddess forms such as Kali or Durga). In Srikula practice, moreover, every aspect of the Goddess – whether malignant or gentle – is identified with Lalita.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks199259-60_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks199259-60-73">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Srikula adepts most often worship Lalita using the abstract <i>Sri Chakra</i> <a href="/wiki/Yantra" title="Yantra">yantra</a>, which is regarded as her subtle form. The Sri Chakra can be visually rendered either as a two-dimensional diagram (whether drawn temporarily as part of the worship ritual, or permanently engraved in metal) or in the three-dimensional, pyramidal form known as the <i>Sri Meru</i>. It is not uncommon to find a <i>Sri Chakra</i> or <i>Sri Meru</i> installed in South Indian temples, because – as modern practitioners assert – "there is no disputing that this is the highest form of Devi and that some of the practice can be done openly. But what you see in the temples is not the <i>srichakra</i> worship you see when it is done privately."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[c]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Srividya <i><a href="/wiki/Parampara" title="Parampara">paramparas</a></i> can be further broadly subdivided into two streams, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaula" title="Kaula">Kaula</a></i> (a <i><a href="/wiki/Vamachara" title="Vamachara">vamamarga</a></i> practice) and the <i>Samaya</i> (a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dakshinachara" title="Dakshinachara">dakshinamarga</a></i> practice). The <i>Kaula</i> or <i>Kaulachara</i>, first appeared as a coherent ritual system in the 8th century in central India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2003219_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2003219-76">[73]</a></sup> and its most revered theorist is the 18th-century philosopher <a href="/wiki/Bhaskararaya" title="Bhaskararaya">Bhaskararaya</a>, widely considered "the best exponent of Shakta philosophy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhattacharyya1996209_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharyya1996209-77">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Samaya</i> or <i>Samayacharya</i> finds its roots in the work of the 16th-century commentator Lakshmidhara, and is "fiercely puritanical [in its] attempts to reform Tantric practice in ways that bring it in line with high-caste <a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">brahmanical</a> norms."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks199028_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks199028-78">[75]</a></sup> Many Samaya practitioners explicitly deny being either Shakta or Tantric, though scholars argues that their cult remains technically both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks199028_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks199028-78">[75]</a></sup> The Samaya-Kaula division marks "an old dispute within Hindu Tantrism,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrooks199028_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks199028-78">[75]</a></sup> and one that is vigorously debated to this day.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kalikula:_family_of_Kali">Kalikula: family of Kali</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Kalikula: family of Kali">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kali_lithograph.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="894" data-file-width="664" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Kali_lithograph.jpg/170px-Kali_lithograph.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Kali_lithograph.jpg/255px-Kali_lithograph.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Kali_lithograph.jpg/340px-Kali_lithograph.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kali_lithograph.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Kali in her <i>Dakshina Kali</i> form</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <i>Kalikula</i> (family of <i>Kali</i>) form of Shaktism is most dominant in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, northern and eastern India, and is most widely prevalent in <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, as well as parts of <a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and some parts of <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>. <i>Kalikula</i> lineages focus upon the Devi as the source of wisdom (<i>vidya</i>) and liberation (<i><a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a></i>). They generally stand "in opposition to the brahmanic tradition," which they view as "overly conservative and denying the experiential part of religion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDanieln.d._79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDanieln.d.-79">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main deities of Kalikula are <i>Kali</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Chandi" title="Chandi">Chandi</a></i> and <i>Durga</i>. Other goddesses that enjoy veneration are <i>Tara</i> and all the other <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahavidyas" title="Mahavidyas">Mahavidyas</a></i> as well as regional goddesses such as <i><a href="/wiki/Manasa" title="Manasa">Manasa</a></i>, the snake goddess, and <i><a href="/wiki/Shitala" title="Shitala">Sitala</a></i>, the smallpox goddess – all of them, again, considered aspects of the Divine Mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDanieln.d._79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDanieln.d.-79">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> devi is mainly worshipped as Kali, Bhawani, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matrika" title="Matrika">Matrika</a> and <a href="/wiki/Navadurga" title="Navadurga">Navadurga</a>.There are many shakti peeth in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> including the main shakti peeth <a href="/wiki/Guhyeshwari_Temple" title="Guhyeshwari Temple">Guhyeshwari Temple</a> of Guhyeshwari Devi also called as Guhekali Bhagawati on the bank of holy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bagmati" title="Bagmati">Bagmati</a> river. She is one of the important deity in kalikula. Two major centers of Shaktism in West Bengal are <a href="/wiki/Kalighat" title="Kalighat">Kalighat</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcutta" title="Calcutta">Calcutta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tarapith" title="Tarapith">Tarapith</a> in <a href="/wiki/Birbhum_district" title="Birbhum district">Birbhum district</a>. In Calcutta, emphasis is on devotion (<i>bhakti</i>) to the goddess as <i>Kali</i>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>She is "the loving mother who protects her children and whose fierceness guards them. She is outwardly frightening – with dark skin, pointed teeth, and a necklace of skulls – but inwardly beautiful. She can guarantee a good rebirth or great religious insight, and her worship is often communal – especially at festivals, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Kali_Puja" title="Kali Puja">Kali Puja</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Durga_Puja" title="Durga Puja">Durga Puja</a></i>. Worship may involve contemplation of the devotee's union with or love of the goddess, visualization of her form, chanting [of her] <i>mantras</i>, prayer before her image or <i>yantra</i>, and giving [of] offerings."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDanieln.d._79-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDanieln.d.-79">[76]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>At Tarapith, Devi's manifestation as <i>Tara</i> ("She Who Saves") or <i>Ugratara</i> ("Fierce Tara") is ascendant, as the goddess who gives liberation (<i>kaivalyadayini</i>). [...] The forms of <i>sadhana</i> performed here are more <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yogic" title="Yogic">yogic</a></i> and <i>tantric</i> than devotional, and they often involve sitting alone at the [cremation] ground, surrounded by ash and bone. There are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamanic" title="Shamanic">shamanic</a> elements associated with the Tarapith tradition, including "conquest of the goddess', exorcism, trance, and control of spirits."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDanieln.d._79-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDanieln.d.-79">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The philosophical and devotional underpinning of all such ritual, however, remains a pervasive vision of the Devi as supreme, absolute divinity. As expressed by the nineteenth-century saint <a href="/wiki/Ramakrishna" title="Ramakrishna">Ramakrishna</a>, one of the most influential figures in modern Bengali Shaktism:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Kali is none other than Brahman. That which is called Brahman is really Kali. She is the Primal Energy. When that Energy remains inactive, I call It Brahman, and when It creates, preserves, or destroys, I call It Shakti or Kali. What you call Brahman I call Kali. Brahman and Kali are not different. They are like fire and its power to burn: if one thinks of fire one must think of its power to burn. If one recognizes Kali one must also recognize Brahman; again, if one recognizes Brahman one must recognize Kali. Brahman and Its Power are identical. It is Brahman whom I address as Shakti or Kali.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENikhilananda2000734_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENikhilananda2000734-80">[77]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals">Festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shaktas celebrate most major Hindu festivals, as well as a huge variety of local, temple- or deity-specific observances. A few of the more important events are listed below:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPattanaik2000103-109_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPattanaik2000103-109-81">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Navratri">Navratri</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Navratri">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Navratri" title="Navratri">Navratri</a></div>
<p>The most important Shakta festival is <i><a href="/wiki/Navratri" title="Navratri">Navratri</a></i> (lit., "Festival of Nine Nights"), also known as "Sharad Navratri" because it falls during the Hindu month of <a href="/wiki/Sharad" title="Sharad">Sharad</a> (October/November). This festival – often taken together with the following tenth day, known as <i>Dusshera</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Vijayadashami" title="Vijayadashami">Vijayadashami</a></i> – celebrates the goddess Durga's victory over a series of powerful demons described in the <i><a href="/wiki/Devi_Mahatmya" title="Devi Mahatmya">Devi Mahatmya</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsley198795-115_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsley198795-115-82">[79]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a>, the last four days of Navaratri are called <a href="/wiki/Durga_Puja" title="Durga Puja">Durga Puja</a>, and mark one episode in particular: Durga's iconic slaying of <a href="/wiki/Mahishasura" title="Mahishasura">Mahishasura</a> (lit., the "Buffalo Demon").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Hindus of all denominations celebrate the autumn Navratri festival, Shaktas also celebrate two additional Navratris – one in the spring and one in the summer. The spring festival is known as <i>Vasanta Navaratri</i> or <i>Chaitra Navatri</i>, and celebrated in the Hindu month of <a href="/wiki/Chaitra" title="Chaitra">Chaitra</a> (March/April). Srividya lineages dedicate this festival to Devi's form as the goddess <a href="/wiki/Tripura_Sundari" title="Tripura Sundari">Lalita</a>. The summer festival is called <i>Ashada Navaratri</i>, as it is held during the Hindu month of <a href="/wiki/Ashadha" title="Ashadha">Ashadha</a> (June/July). The <a href="/wiki/Vaishno_Devi" title="Vaishno Devi">Vaishno Devi</a> temple in <a href="/wiki/Jammu" title="Jammu">Jammu</a>, with Vaishno Devi considered an aspect of Durga, celebrates Navaratri.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsley198795-115_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsley198795-115-82">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> <i>Ashada Navaratri</i>, on the other hand, is considered particularly auspicious for devotees of the boar-headed goddess <a href="/wiki/Varahi" title="Varahi">Varahi</a>, one of the seven Matrikas named in the <i>Devi Mahatmya</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vasant_Panchami">Vasant Panchami</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Vasant Panchami">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saraswati_Pooja" title="Saraswati Pooja">Saraswati Pooja</a></div>
<p>Fifth day of Magha Gupta Navratri is very important for all branches of Shakta-pantha. Specially in Vindhyachal mahashakti peetham, thousands of chandipatha and other secret rituals performed this day to please Aadishakti. This is the festival of union of Shakti &amp; Shiv (Shiva-Shiv). On the same basis Shiva-Shiv Sammoh is formed by Awadhoot Kripanandnath at Awadhoot Ashram, Vindhyachal in 1980.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diwali_and_others">Diwali and others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Diwali and others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diwali" title="Diwali">Diwali</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lakshmi_Puja" title="Lakshmi Puja">Lakshmi Puja</a> is a part of Durga Puja celebrations by Shaktas, where Laksmi symbolizes the goddess of abundance and autumn harvest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsley198733_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsley198733-86">[83]</a></sup> Lakshmi's biggest festival, however, is <i><a href="/wiki/Diwali" title="Diwali">Diwali</a></i> (or <i>Deepavali</i>; the "Festival of Lights"), a major Hindu holiday celebrated across India and in Nepal as Tihar. In North India, Diwali marks the beginning of the traditional New Year, and is held on the night of the new moon in the Hindu month of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kartika_(month)" title="Kartika (month)">Kartik</a> (usually October or November). Shaktas (and many non-Shaktas) celebrate it as another Lakshmi Puja, placing small oil lamps outside their homes and praying for the goddess's blessings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> Diwali coincides with the celebration of Kali Puja, popular in Bengal, and some Shakta traditions focus their worship on Devi as Kali rather than Lakshmi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:India_Meenakshi_Temple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1996" data-file-width="2304" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/India_Meenakshi_Temple.jpg/220px-India_Meenakshi_Temple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/India_Meenakshi_Temple.jpg/330px-India_Meenakshi_Temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/India_Meenakshi_Temple.jpg/440px-India_Meenakshi_Temple.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:India_Meenakshi_Temple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Gopuram" title="Gopuram">gopuram</a> (tower) of the Meenakshi Amman Temple, a Shakta temple at Madurai, Tamil Nadu, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, which was nominated in the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Seven_Wonders_of_the_World" title="New Seven Wonders of the World">New Seven Wonders of the World</a>" competition in 2004.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Jagaddhatri" title="Jagaddhatri">Jagaddhatri</a> Puja</i> is celebrated on the last four days of the Navaratis, following Kali Puja. It is very similar to Durga Puja in its details and observance, and is especially popular in Bengal and some other parts of Eastern India. <i>Gauri Puja</i> is performed on the fifth day after <a href="/wiki/Ganesh_Chaturthi" title="Ganesh Chaturthi">Ganesh Chaturthi</a>, during <a href="/wiki/Ganesha" title="Ganesha">Ganesha</a> Puja in Western India, to celebrate the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Parvati" title="Parvati">Gauri</a>, Mother of Ganesha where she brings her son back home.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Major Shakta temple festivals are <i>Meenakshi Kalyanam</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ambubachi_Mela" title="Ambubachi Mela">Ambubachi Mela</a></i>. The <i>Meenakshi Kalyanam</i> is a part of the <a href="/wiki/Chithirai_Thiruvizha" title="Chithirai Thiruvizha">Chithirai Thiruvizha</a> festival in <a href="/wiki/Madurai" title="Madurai">Madurai</a> around April/May, one of the largest festivals in South India, celebrating the wedding of goddess <a href="/wiki/Minakshi" title="Minakshi">Meenakshi</a> (Parvati) and Shiva. The festival is one where both the Vaishnava and Shaiva communities join the celebrations, because Vishnu gives away his sister Meenakshi in marriage to Shiva.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Ambubachi_Mela" title="Ambubachi Mela">Ambubachi Mela</a></i> or Ameti is a celebration of the menstruation of the goddess, by hundreds of thousands of devotees, in a festival held in June/July (during the monsoon season) at <a href="/wiki/Kamakhya_Temple" title="Kamakhya Temple">Kamakhya Temple</a>, Guwahati, Assam. Here the Devi is worshiped in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Yoni" title="Yoni">yoni</a>-like stone, and the site is one of Shakta Pitha or pilgrimage sites in Shaktism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_sacrifice">Animal sacrifice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Animal sacrifice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:288px;max-width:288px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:82px;max-width:82px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Durga_slaying_the_Buffalo_demon_Mahisuramardini_LACMA_M.70.42.8.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1767" data-file-width="982" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Durga_slaying_the_Buffalo_demon_Mahisuramardini_LACMA_M.70.42.8.jpg/80px-Durga_slaying_the_Buffalo_demon_Mahisuramardini_LACMA_M.70.42.8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Durga_slaying_the_Buffalo_demon_Mahisuramardini_LACMA_M.70.42.8.jpg/120px-Durga_slaying_the_Buffalo_demon_Mahisuramardini_LACMA_M.70.42.8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Durga_slaying_the_Buffalo_demon_Mahisuramardini_LACMA_M.70.42.8.jpg/160px-Durga_slaying_the_Buffalo_demon_Mahisuramardini_LACMA_M.70.42.8.jpg 2x" width="80"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Immolation_Sacrifice,_Mouh_Boli,_Durga_Puja.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1858" data-file-width="2584" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Immolation_Sacrifice%2C_Mouh_Boli%2C_Durga_Puja.jpg/200px-Immolation_Sacrifice%2C_Mouh_Boli%2C_Durga_Puja.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Immolation_Sacrifice%2C_Mouh_Boli%2C_Durga_Puja.jpg/300px-Immolation_Sacrifice%2C_Mouh_Boli%2C_Durga_Puja.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Immolation_Sacrifice%2C_Mouh_Boli%2C_Durga_Puja.jpg/400px-Immolation_Sacrifice%2C_Mouh_Boli%2C_Durga_Puja.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">In Shaktism mythology, Durga slays an evil buffalo demon (left, 18th century statue), a story enacted by some of its devotees (right) in <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Durga_puja" title="Durga puja">Durga puja</a> festival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_Christopher_John_2004_83_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller_Christopher_John_2004_83-91">[88]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shaktism tradition practices animal sacrifice to revere goddesses such as <a href="/wiki/Kali" title="Kali">Kali</a> in many parts of India but particularly in the eastern states of India and Nepal. This is either an actual animal, or a vegetal or sweet dish substitute considered equivalent to the animal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> In many cases, Shaktism devotees consider animal sacrifice distasteful, practice alternate means of expressing devotion while respecting the views of others in their tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Nepal, West Bengal, Odisha and Assam, animal sacrifices are performed at Shakti temples, particular to mark the legend of goddess Durga slaying the buffalo demon. This involves slaying of a <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chicken</a> or a male <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a>. This practice is rare among Hindus, outside this region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_Christopher_John_2004_83_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller_Christopher_John_2004_83-91">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Bengal, animal sacrifice follows the guidelines in texts such as Mahanirvana Tantra are followed in selecting the animal, then a priest offers a prayer to the animal, then recites the <a href="/wiki/Gayatri_Mantra" title="Gayatri Mantra">Gayatri Mantra</a> in its ear before killing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup> The meat of the sacrificed animal is then eaten by the Shakta devotee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_Christopher_John_2004_83_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller_Christopher_John_2004_83-91">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Nepal, animal sacrifice <i>en masse</i> occurs during the three-day-long <a href="/wiki/Gadhimai_festival" title="Gadhimai festival">Gadhimai festival</a>. In 2009 it was speculated that more than 250,000 animals were sacrificed during this event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Odisha, during the <a href="/wiki/Bali_Jatra" title="Bali Jatra">Bali Jatra</a>, Shaktism devotees sacrifice male goats to the goddess <a href="/wiki/Samaleswari" title="Samaleswari">Samaleswari</a> in her temple in <a href="/wiki/Sambalpur" title="Sambalpur">Sambalpur</a>, Orissa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Rajput" title="Rajput">Rajput</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a> worship their weapons and horses on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navaratri" title="Navaratri">Navratri</a>, and formerly offered a sacrifice of a goat to a goddess revered as Kuldevi – a practice that continues in some places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goat_sacrifice_to_Shilamata_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goat_sacrifice_to_Shilamata-100">[97]</a></sup> The ritual requires slaying of the animal with a single stroke. In the past this ritual was considered a rite of passage into manhood and readiness as a warrior. The ritual is directed by a priest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup> The <i>Kuldevi</i> among these Rajput communities is a warrior-pativrata guardian goddess, with local legends tracing reverence for her during Rajput-Muslim wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Animal Sacrifice of a buffalo or goat, particularly during smallpox epidemics, has been practiced in parts of South India. The sacrificed animal is dedicated to a goddess, and is probably related to the myth of goddess Kali in Andhra Pradesh, but in Karnataka, the typical goddess is <a href="/wiki/Renuka" title="Renuka">Renuka</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Alf_Hiltebeitel" title="Alf Hiltebeitel">Alf Hiltebeitel</a> – a professor of Religions, History and Human Sciences, these ritual animal sacrifices, with some differences, mirrors goddess-related ritual animal sacrifice found in <a href="/wiki/Gilgamesh" title="Gilgamesh">Gilgamesh</a> epic and in texts of Egyptian, Minoan and Greek sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buffalo_sacrifice_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buffalo_sacrifice-103">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 19th-century through the early 20th-century, Indian laborers were shipped by the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> into colonial mining and plantations operations in the Indian ocean and the Caribbean regions. These included significant number of Shakta devotees. While instances of Shakta animal sacrifice during Kali puja in the Caribbean islands were recorded between 1850s to 1920s, these were relatively uncommon when compared to other rituals such as temple prayers, community dancing and fire walking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Shaktism_versus_other_Hindu_traditions">Shaktism versus other Hindu traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Shaktism versus other Hindu traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1849_engraving_of_%22the_Hindoo_Goddess_Karle%22.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="471" data-file-width="557" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/1849_engraving_of_%22the_Hindoo_Goddess_Karle%22.JPG/170px-1849_engraving_of_%22the_Hindoo_Goddess_Karle%22.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/1849_engraving_of_%22the_Hindoo_Goddess_Karle%22.JPG/255px-1849_engraving_of_%22the_Hindoo_Goddess_Karle%22.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/1849_engraving_of_%22the_Hindoo_Goddess_Karle%22.JPG/340px-1849_engraving_of_%22the_Hindoo_Goddess_Karle%22.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1849_engraving_of_%22the_Hindoo_Goddess_Karle%22.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"The Hindoo Goddess Karle", an illustration from <i>Dr. Scudder's Tales for Little Readers About the Heathen</i>, by Dr. John Scudder (London, 1849).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shaktism has at times been dismissed as a superstitious, black magic-infested practice that hardly qualifies as a true religion at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUrban2003_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUrban2003-105">[102]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2016)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2003_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2003-106">[103]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (October 2016)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A representative criticism of this sort issued from an Indian scholar in the 1920s:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Tantras are the Bible of Shaktism, identifying all Force with the female principle in nature and teaching an undue adoration of the wives of Shiva and Vishnu to the neglect of their male counterparts. It is certain that a vast number of the inhabitants of India are guided in their daily life by Tantrik [sic] teaching, and are in bondage to the gross superstitions inculcated in these writings. And indeed it can scarcely be doubted that Shaktism is Hinduism arrived at its worst and most corrupt stage of development."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKapoor2002157_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKapoor2002157-107">[104]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The tantra practices are secretive, subject to speculations and criticism. Scholars variously attribute such criticism to ignorance, misunderstanding or sectarian bias on the part of some observers, as well as unscrupulous practices by some Shaktas. These are some of the reasons many Hindus question the relevance and historicity of Tantra to their tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite2003262_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite2003262-108">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hughurban_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-hughurban-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beyond tantra, the Shakta sub-traditions subscribe to various philosophies, are similar in some aspects and differ in others. These traditions compare with Shaivism, Shaktism and Smartism as follows:</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_and_influence">Temples and influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Temples and influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Category:Shakti_temples" title="Category:Shakti temples">List of Shakti Temples</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakti_Peethas" title="Shakti Peethas">Shakti Peethas</a></div>
