<div style="position:absolute;left:-5px;top:-5px;line-height:0"><img alt="Guwahati" data-file-height="64" data-file-width="64" height="9" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Blue_pog.svg/9px-Blue_pog.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Blue_pog.svg/14px-Blue_pog.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Blue_pog.svg/18px-Blue_pog.svg.png 2x" title="Guwahati" width="9"/></div>
<div style="font-size:90%;line-height:110%;position:absolute;width:6em;bottom:6px;left:-3em;text-align:center"><span style="padding:1px"><b><a href="/wiki/Guwahati" title="Guwahati">Guwahati</a></b></span></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:India_location_map_disputed.svg" title="File:India location map disputed.svg"></a></div>
The map depicts location of Shakti Peethas in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, major (blue) and minor (red) .</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shakta temples are found all over South Asia. Many towns, villages and geographic landmarks are named for various forms of the Devi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPattanaik2000110-114_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPattanaik2000110-114-130">[127]</a></sup> Major pilgrimage sites of Shaktism are called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakti_Peethas" title="Shakti Peethas">Shakti Peethas</a>", literally "Seats of the Devi". These vary from four to fifty one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhattacharyya1996171_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharyya1996171-131">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some Shakta temples are also found in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFell_McDermett1998281-305_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFell_McDermett1998281-305-132">[129]</a></sup> Examples in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> include the <i>Kali Mandir</i> in <a href="/wiki/Laguna_Beach,_California" title="Laguna Beach, California">Laguna Beach, California</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sri_Rajarajeswari_Peetam" title="Sri Rajarajeswari Peetam">Sri Rajarajeswari Peetam</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup> a <i><a href="/wiki/Srividya" title="Srividya">Srividya</a></i> temple in rural <a href="/wiki/Rush,_New_York" title="Rush, New York">Rush, New York</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDempsey2006_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDempsey2006-135">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminists</a> and participants in <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> spirituality who are attracted to goddess worship", suggest Shaktism is a "symbol of wholeness and healing, associated especially with repressed female power and sexuality." However, these are adaptions and do not share Shakta theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFell_McDermett1998281-305_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFell_McDermett1998281-305-132">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhism">Buddhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There has been a significant sharing of ideas, ritual grammar and concepts between Tantric Buddhism (<a href="/wiki/Vajrayana" title="Vajrayana">Vajrayana</a> tradition) found in Nepal and Tibet and the Tantric Shakta tradition of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalal332_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-dalal332-137">[134]</a></sup> Both movements cherish female deities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keul2012p119_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keul2012p119-138">[135]</a></sup> view the female creativity as the power behind the universe, and the feminine as the ontological primary. According to Miranda Shaw, "the confluence of Buddhism and Shaktism is such that Tantric Buddhism could properly be called Shakta Buddhism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Aurangabad_Caves" title="Aurangabad Caves">Aurangabad Caves</a> about 100 kilometers from the <a href="/wiki/Ajanta_Caves" title="Ajanta Caves">Ajanta Caves</a>, dated to the 6th to 7th-century CE, show Buddhist Matrikas (mother goddesses of Shaktism) next to the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brancaccio203_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brancaccio203-140">[137]</a></sup> Other goddesses in these caves include Durga. The goddess iconography in these Buddhist caves is close, but not identical to the Hindu Shakta tradition. The "seven goddess mothers" are found in other Buddhist caves and literature, such as their discussion in the Buddhist text <i>Manjusrimulakalpa</i> and <i>Vairocanabhisambodhi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brancaccio203_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brancaccio203-140">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:608px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:India_settentrionale,_saptamatrika,_X_sec.JPG" title="Matrika – mother goddesses – are found in both Shakta-Hinduism and Vajrayana-Buddhism."><img alt="" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="2436" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/India_settentrionale%2C_saptamatrika%2C_X_sec.JPG/600px-India_settentrionale%2C_saptamatrika%2C_X_sec.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/India_settentrionale%2C_saptamatrika%2C_X_sec.JPG/900px-India_settentrionale%2C_saptamatrika%2C_X_sec.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/India_settentrionale%2C_saptamatrika%2C_X_sec.JPG/1200px-India_settentrionale%2C_saptamatrika%2C_X_sec.JPG 2x" width="600"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:India_settentrionale,_saptamatrika,_X_sec.JPG" title="File:India settentrionale, saptamatrika, X sec.JPG"></a></div>
<i>Matrika</i> – mother goddesses – are found in both Shakta-Hinduism and Vajrayana-Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keul2012p119_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keul2012p119-138">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[139]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jainism">Jainism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Jainism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, ideas similar to Shaktism tradition are found, such as the Vidyadevis and the Shasanadevis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalal332_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-dalal332-137">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikhism">Sikhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sikhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The secondary scripture of Sikhs, <i><a href="/wiki/Dasam_Granth" title="Dasam Granth">Dasam Granth</a></i> attributed to <a href="/wiki/Guru_Gobind_Singh" title="Guru Gobind Singh">Guru Gobind Singh</a>, includes numerous sections on Shakta goddesses, particularly <a href="/wiki/Chandi" title="Chandi">Chandi</a> – the fierce warrior form of the Hindu goddess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rinehart2011_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rinehart2011-143">[140]</a></sup> According to Nikky-Guninder Kaur Singh – a professor of Religious Studies, the stories about goddess Durga in the <i>Dasam Granth</i> are reworkings of ancient Shakti mythologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup> A significant part of this Sikh scripture is based on the teachings in the Shakta text <i>Devi Mahatmya</i> found in the <i><a href="/wiki/Markandeya_Purana" title="Markandeya Purana">Markandeya Purana</a></i> of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_ancient_religions">Other ancient religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shaktism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other ancient religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some Westerners<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> believe that many central concepts of Shaktism – including aspects of kundalini yoga as well as goddess worship – were once "common to the Hindu, <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Chaldean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> civilizations," but were largely superseded in the West, as well as the Near and Middle East, with the rise of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religion" title="Abrahamic religion">Abrahamic religions</a>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Of these four great ancient civilizations, working knowledge of the inner forces of enlightenment has survived on a mass scale only in India. Only in India has the inner tradition of the Goddess endured. This is the reason the teachings of India are so precious. They offer us a glimpse of what our own ancient wisdom must have been. The Indians have preserved our lost heritage. [...] Today it is up to us to locate and restore the tradition of the living Goddess. We would do well to begin our search in India, where for not one moment in all of human history have the children of the living Goddess forgotten their Divine Mother.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Linda_Johnsen" title="Linda Johnsen">Linda Johnsen</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnsen2002176.2C_181_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnsen2002176.2C_181-146">[143]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
